<script>
	function goToTopic(topic) {
		var topicMap = applySpecialCharacterMapping(topic); 
		var url = "/xpl/topicbrowse.jsp?topic=" + escape(topicMap);
		window.top.location.href = url;
	}
</script>

The antenna possesses a two-sided structure that is printed on an FR4 substrate and fed by a 50-Â¿ microstrip line. By properly using helical strips and vias, the antenna size can be reduced to a small volume of 10 (L) Ã 9.5 (W) Ã 0.8 (H) mm<sup>3</sup> to be easily integrated within various portable devices. A miniaturized quasi-lumped circuit is also designed to achieve a good impedance matching between the antenna and the tag chip. Experimental results demonstrate that the antenna has a suitable operating band about 914-939 MHz and also nearly omnidirectional radiation patterns with proper gain. Moreover, the proposed compact tag antenna, capable of achieving a maximum readable range about 5.6 m with an effective isotropic radiated power (EIRP) equal to 4 W, is well suitable for RFID applications.</div>

<div class="section" id="sec1"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION I.</div><h2>Introduction</h2></div><p>In recent years, the radio frequency identification (RFID) techniques with automatic wireless data access have rapidly earned a lot of attention in various commercial purposes, such as supply chain managements and retail store applications <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref1" id="context_ref_1_1">[1]</a>. Compared to the lower frequency tags (LF and HF bands) already suffering from limited operation range, the UHF RFID tags are capable of achieving farther read range and higher data transfer rate <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_1">[2]</a>. Nevertheless, those UHF RFID tags need not only good impedance matching to attain efficient power transfer between the antenna and the tag chip, but also a compact size to be flexibly integrated within different modern portable devices. Generally, tag antennas based on half-wavelength dipole were developed to provide a large coverage <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref3" id="context_ref_3_1">[3]</a>â<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_1">[4]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref5" id="context_ref_5_1">[5]</a>, but they were too large for some practical applications as well. To further possess a small tag design, several planar antennas using loop shape to miniaturize antenna size have been studied in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref6" id="context_ref_6_1">[6]</a>â<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref7" id="context_ref_7_1">[7]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref8" id="context_ref_8_1">[8]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref9" id="context_ref_9_1">[9]</a>. However, also note that these compact designs all reveal a shorter reading range owing to lower radiation performance.</p><p>In this letter, we propose a printed monopole tag antenna that merely occupies a compact size of 10 (<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-1-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-1" role="math" style="width: 0.664em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.604em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.384em 1000.54em 2.405em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-2"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-3"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-4"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-5" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">L</span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.87em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-1">{\rm L}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>)Ã9.5 (<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-2-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-6" role="math" style="width: 1.144em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.024em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.384em 1001.02em 2.405em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-7"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-8"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-9"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-10" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">W</span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.937em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-2">{\rm W}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>)Ã0.8 (<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-3-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-11" role="math" style="width: 0.844em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.724em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.384em 1000.72em 2.405em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-12"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-13"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-14"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-15" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">H</span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.87em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-3">{\rm H}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>) mm<sup>3</sup> to be flexibly embedded inside different portable devices, such as a mobile phone and a laptop computer. To reduce antenna size, this design to resonate at RFID UHF band is implemented using helical strips and vias. Also, with the help of an additional miniaturized matching circuit using quasi-lumped elements, good impedance matching between the antenna and the tag chip can be obtained. Unlike those designs presented in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref3" id="context_ref_3_1">[3]</a>â<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_1">[4]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref5" id="context_ref_5_1">[5]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref6" id="context_ref_6_1">[6]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref7" id="context_ref_7_1">[7]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref8" id="context_ref_8_1">[8]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref9" id="context_ref_9_1">[9]</a>, both the tag antenna and its matching circuit may be easily printed by general printed circuit board (PCB) process, so the fabrication cost can be further decreased. Details of the tag antenna design are then presented, and a prototype of the proposed antenna has been constructed and experimentally studied. Moreover, a maximum readable range of the fabricated tag will be carefully tested in an anechoic chamber with a commercial RFID reader.</p></div>

width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.937em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-6">{\rm W}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>) mm<sup>2</sup>. The antenna, fabricated on a 0.8-mm-thick FR4 substrate with dielectric constant <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-7-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-29" role="math" style="width: 3.907em; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.901em; left: 0.483em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-33"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-34"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-35" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">r</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-36" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.303em;">=</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-37" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.303em;">4.4</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.263em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.07em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-7">\varepsilon_{r} = 4.4</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and loss tangent <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-8-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-38" role="math" style="width: 5.829em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 5.228em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1005.17em 2.405em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-39"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-40" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">tan</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-41"></span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-42" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic; padding-left: 0.183em;">Î´<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.003em;"></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-43" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.303em;">=</span><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-44" style="padding-left: 0.303em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-45"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-46" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">0.02</span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.937em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-8">\tan \delta= {0.02}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, consists of helical strips horizontally printed on the front and back sides. Here, these strips are serially connected by using the vias with a diameter of 0.4 mm. In such a structure, the resonant frequency can be determined by the total length in regard to the helical strips and vias. Accordingly, this total length is about a quarter-wavelength at the frequency of 925 MHz. Therefore, the antenna size can be further well reduced. For the ground plane, it is selected to be 100 mm in length and 40 mm in width, which are reasonable dimensions for general portable devices. In addition, this tag antenna has been modeled and constructed by employing an electromagnetic (EM) software package HFSS, whose electrical properties and radiation performance are also simulated and analyzed.

To tackle this issue, a miniaturized quasi-lumped circuit is further developed to make an excellent impedance matching between the antenna and the tag chip. Here, the input impedance of the antenna is simulated to be <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-21-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-179" role="math" style="width: 5.228em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 4.688em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1004.63em 2.646em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-180"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-181" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">(</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-182" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">26</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-183" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.243em;">+</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-184" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic; padding-left: 0.243em;">j</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-185" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">3</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-186" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">)</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-187" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">Î©</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.27em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-21">(26+ j 3) \Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula> at the frequency of 925 MHz. For maximum power transfer, a matching circuit using meandered line is thus designed, which is composed of a series inductance of 12.3 nH and a shunt inductance of 12.5 nH.</p><p>Furthermore, there is a small empty space right and adjacent to the tag antenna. In <a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">TableÂ I</a>, comparisons of the proposed tag antenna with those presented in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref6" id="context_ref_6_2">[6]</a>â<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref7" id="context_ref_7_2">[7]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref8" id="context_ref_8_2">[8]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref9" id="context_ref_9_2">[9]</a> on antenna size are given. It is noted that here the antenna size for our design means an area including the tag antenna and the matching circuit. As a result, significant reduction in size has been achieved with our tag antenna. As for practical application, to further ensure the antenna performance, the ground plane size is suggested at least a quarter-wavelength for the operating frequency.

<div class="section" id="sec3"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION III.</div><h2>Results and Discussions</h2></div><p>A fabricated prototype shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 2</a> for the proposed tag antenna was implemented, which was mounted at the top edge of the ground plane and also connected to the quasi-lumped matching circuit. The measured results were performed by using a vector network analyzer (Agilent PNA 8362B). <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 3</a> illustrates the simulated and measured impedances against frequency for the tag antenna with the matching circuit, where good agreements between them have been achieved. We can also see that the simulated and measured impedances are respectively equal to <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-37-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-344" role="math" style="width: 6.309em; Thus, referring to these results, the power reflection coefficient (PRC) curves of the tag antenna can be calculated in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig4" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 4</a>. With <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-39-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-362" role="math" style="width: 2.045em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.805em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.384em 1001.75em 2.405em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-363"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-364" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">â</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-365" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">10</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.87em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-39">-10 </script></tex-math></inline-formula>dB return loss, the simulated and measured operating frequency bands have been determined to be about 912â932 and 914â939 MHz, corresponding to 2.2% and 2.7%, respectively. A small discrepancy between the simulations and measurements may be mainly attributed to the effects caused by the connector and feeding cable in the measurements. Also note that the simulated and measured bandwidths both cover the RFID UHF band well.

width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.27em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-40">(26+ j 3) \Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula> near to a pure resistance, the general 50-<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-41-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-375" role="math" style="width: 0.844em; top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-376"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-377" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">Î©</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.937em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-41">\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula> far-field chamber can be used to measure the radiation patterns for our design. Here, the return loss between the antenna and the chamber system is calculated to be 10 dB. This further means that 90% energy can be fed into the tag antenna for radiation and also 10% energy is lost. According to this, the measured results for the radiation patterns should be theoretically divided by a factor of 0.9 to attain the actual performance of the tag antenna. Thus, <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig5" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 5</a> plots the measured radiation patterns in <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-42-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-378" role="math" style="width: 1.144em; Good coverage is therefore reached as well.</p><p>To further verify the antenna performance, the experiments about the maximum reading range for the fabricated tag were performed in an anechoic chamber, as depicted in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig7" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 7</a>. An Impinj Speedway RFID reader with an operating frequency range from 922 to 928 MHz and a maximum output power of 30 dBm has been used for those tests. In those measurements, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-55-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-502" role="math" style="width: 0.604em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.544em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.264em 1000.54em 2.345em -999.997em); height: 1px; width: 0.003em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.937em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-55">d</script></tex-math></inline-formula> was fixed at 3.6 m, and the measured <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-56-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-505" role="math" style="width: 2.165em; clip: rect(3.186em 1000.78em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-508" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">P<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.123em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.901em; left: 0.664em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-509"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-510"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-511" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">min</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.197em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.07em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-56">P _{\min}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> was obtained about 27.5 dBm. Therefore, if the EIRP of the reader is set to 4 W, the maximum readable range for our design will be 5.9 m. This result is also better than those compact tags presented in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref6" id="context_ref_6_3">[6]</a>â<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref7" id="context_ref_7_3">[7]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref8" id="context_ref_8_3">[8]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref9" id="context_ref_9_3">[9]</a>, so that the proposed tag antenna is more suitable for applications in various commercial purposes.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig5"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5423326/5456169/5456169-fig-5-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5423326/5456169/5456169-fig-5-small.gif" alt="Figure 5" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 5. </b><fig><p>Measured radiation patterns at 925 MHz for the proposed tag antenna.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/5456169/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>

(a) Measurement setup. (b) Chamber environment.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/5456169/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><p></p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec4"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION IV.</div><h2>Conclusion</h2></div><p>A novel miniature monopole tag antenna with a quasi-lumped matching circuit for UHF RFID applications has been presented and studied in this letter. Compared to other tag designs, the proposed structure is quite compact and also well suitable for general PCB procedure to fabrication, so it can be easily integrated within various portable devices. In such a small tag design, both good coverage and stable radiation performance can be still attained. Experimental results also demonstrate that the maximum reading range of the fabricated tag is about 5.6 m with an EIRP equal to 4 W. For these reasons, the proposed tag antenna is a promising solution to satisfy more identification purposes for RFID system operation.</p></div>
</div></div>

<script>

function getCookie(name) {
	var nameEQ = name + "=";
	var ca = document.cookie.split(';');
	for(var i=0;i &lt; ca.length;i++) {
		var c = ca[i];
		while (c.charAt(0)==' ') c = c.substring(1,c.length);
		if (c.indexOf(nameEQ) == 0) return c.substring(nameEQ.length,c.length);
	}
	return null;
}

	if(getCookie('legacyUserName')!=null)
	{
		if(IBP_WS_ENABLED_FLAG){
		Modal.refreshLegacyAccountTransition('/xpl/mwLegacyAccountTransition.jsp');
		}
	}
});

</script>

The antenna possesses a two-sided structure that is printed on an FR4 substrate and fed by a 50-Â¿ microstrip line.
